        function gptClean(gptText) {

                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="0d71eeb449bb49d5aab3304315cfd573"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="0d71eeb449bb49d5aab3304315cfd573">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'bb2b0a27-7a15-4b7b-afac-f0c2ee96ce49'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d175366174" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Carbon materials with large capacity and low potential serve as promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Carbon defects offer active sites for sodium storage but sacrifice reversibility, whereas carbon microcrystals improve conductivity but may suffer from low capacity. Herein, the balance between defects and microcrystals is realized by the molecular chemistry method of cross-linking and defect repairing. Real-time spectroscopic analyses reveal that the decomposition of tetrabromophthalic anhydride (TBPA) molecule induces the dehydrogenation of pitch to release small gas molecules to form pseudo-closed pores, followed by the cross-linking of C═O bonds in TBPA with the dehydrogenated pitch to form C─O─C bonds and thus suppress the excessive microcrystal growth. Meanwhile, the decomposition products of TBPA can&nbsp;also react with the dehydrogenated pitch to repair carbon defects. Thanks to the microstructure balance, the as-synthesized carbon material exhibits a smoother ion mass transfer channel (<i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub> = 0.386&nbsp;nm, <i>L</i>a = 4.56&nbsp;nm) and a larger storage space (<i>V</i><sub>pseudo-closed pore</sub> = 0.086&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) than the pitch carbon (PC, <i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub> = 0.349&nbsp;nm, <i>L</i>a = 5.90&nbsp;nm, <i>V</i><sub>pseudo-closed pore</sub> = 0.041&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>), increasing the capacity from 162.5 to 336.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. This work sheds a chemical light on regulating carbon material microstructure for advanced SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202409313-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202409313-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as a fast-growing member in the energy storage field due to their abundant sodium resources, superior rate performance, and high safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0001_R_d175366166e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0003_R_d175366166e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Anode material is a crucial component determining the energy density of SIBs. Among current anode materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0004_R_d175366166e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0007_R_d175366166e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Ind. Chem. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> carbon materials are of great significance benefitting from their high theoretical capacity, low working potential, structural stability, and abundant precursor sources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0008_R_d175366166e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Energy">8</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0011_R_d175366166e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Efforts have been dedicated to enhancing the sodium storage performance of carbon materials according to the mechanisms of adsorption-insertion, insertion-adsorption, adsorption-filling, or adsorption-insertion-filling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0012_R_d175366166e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0015_R_d175366166e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Sci.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The number of sodium storage active sites in carbon materials is closely related to the pores, defects, and carbon microcrystals. Pore structure in carbon materials can supply sufficient Na<sup>+</sup> storage space. The pores with narrow entrance sizes can induce the pore-filling of Na<sup>+</sup> as a quasi-metallic cluster and contribute to low-voltage plateau capacity. The pore formation is usually influenced by the precursor molecular structure. Adjusting the free radical content in bamboo could effectively control the formation of closed pore structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0016_R_d175366166e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The crystalline cellulose in wood could create closed pores with graphite-like layers via enclosing and shrinking carbon matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0017_R_d175366166e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pore entrance size could be adjusted by depositing carbon on the pore walls around the entrance, thereby promoting desolvation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0018_R_d175366166e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Natl. Sci. Rev.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another approach involves initially forming open pores by chemical etching followed by the pore entrance shrinking via high-temperature treatment and cooling to create closed nanopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0019_R_d175366166e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0022_R_d175366166e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In addition, it is widely accepted that the microcrystalline structure also matters much for the sodium storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0023_R_d175366166e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Science">23</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0025_R_d175366166e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - eScience">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Carbon materials with special microcrystalline structures can synchronously provide fast channels for charge transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> mass transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0026_R_d175366166e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0028_R_d175366166e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It was reported that creating hybrid microcrystalline states through cross-linking between different carbon precursors can disrupt the ordered rearrangement of the carbon structure and expand the interlayer spacing. For instance, the cross-linking of sucrose and lignite coal could produce carbon-based hybrid microcrystals featuring numerous pseudo-graphitic dominant phases and enlarged interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0029_R_d175366166e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Carbon material with a preferable microcrystalline structure could also be obtained using filter paper and mesophase pitch as precursors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0030_R_d175366166e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Pre-oxidation is another effective approach to regulating the carbon microcrystalline structure, while the increased Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier deserves attention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0031_R_d175366166e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Carbon">31</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0034_R_d175366166e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Rare Met.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, an alternative molecular chemical engineering method that promotes the formation of steric hindrance between adjacent aromatic fragments was proposed to regulate the microcrystalline structure to enhance Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0035_R_d175366166e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the sodium storage capacity achieved by modulating the microcrystalline structure alone is still insufficient, thus increasing the density of active sites such as defects is necessary.</p>
         
         <p>Defects in carbon materials can be categorized into two types according to their origin. One is an intrinsic defect such as lattice defect and structural vacancy due to the distortion of crystal lattice or loss of atoms in the precursor itself, while the other one is an extrinsic defect such as heteroatoms or groups introduced by other active components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0036_R_d175366166e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0039_R_d175366166e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Acta Phys. -Chim. Sin.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These defects can effectively adjust the electronic structure of carbon materials, enhance the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup>, and significantly increase the capacity of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0040_R_d175366166e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Carbon Energy">40</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0042_R_d175366166e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In additin to&nbsp;the higher sodium storage potentials, carbon materials with a large number of defects often exhibit lower initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), because the introduced active atoms or groups will increase the irreversible sodium storage caused by the side reactions such as electrolyte decomposition.</p>
         
         <p>The regulation of defects in carbon materials has attracted attention from the carbon anode community. Converting polynuclear aromatic compounds in light volatiles of pitch to carbon coatings with highly regular graphite&nbsp;microcrystals can effectively reduce defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0043_R_d175366166e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Storage Mater.">43</a>, <a href="#smll202409313-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0044_R_d175366166e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Metal element-assisted catalytic carbonization is effective in reducing defects and adjusting the level of graphitization in carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0045_R_d175366166e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">45</a>, <a href="#smll202409313-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0046_R_d175366166e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Carbon Energy">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A specific amount of Mn<sup>2+</sup> (0.004&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>) could facilitate the formation of well-ordered graphene layers and regulate the graphitization at a molecular level for producing high-performance carbon materials (336.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0047_R_d175366166e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, excessive concentrations of Mn<sup>2+</sup> (0.01&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>) could result in the over-graphitization of carbon materials and thus degrade the reversible capacity to 238.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Therefore, maintaining the balance between defects and carbon microcrystals is crucial for efficient Na<sup>+</sup> storage, which has never been reported before.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we present a facile approach to balance defects and microcrystals to enhance Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance through a molecular chemistry method. A balanced carbon material is synthesized using pitch and tetrabromophthalic anhydride (TBPA) as precursor and regulator, respectively. We study the in-situ repair mechanism of carbon defects using advanced characterization such as X-ray diffraction (XRD), Raman, small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS), high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), true density, and thermogravimetry-mass spectrometry (TG-MS). The regulation mechanism of carbon microcrystal growth through the cross-linking between aromatic species and TBPA molecules is investigated by thermogravimetry-differential scanning calorimetry (TG-DSC) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). Accordingly, we propose the balance mechanism involving defect repair and microcrystal growth suppression from the perspective of molecular chemical engineering. The electrochemical performance is evaluated to establish the structure-performance relationship, while the sodium storage mechanism is studied using ex-situ XRD and Raman analysis.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202409313-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202409313-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202409313-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202409313-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Synthesis</h3>
            
            <p>The materials are synthesized by regulating the carbonization of the pitch by TBPA (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0001">1</a>). A series of carbon materials are obtained (PC, PTC11, PTC23, PTC12) according to the ratio of pitch to TBPA (1:0, 1:1, 2:3, 1:2). During the synthesis process, TBPA decomposes to release Br<sup>−</sup> that interacts with hydrogen in pitch, forming HBr gas and then creating pores. The oxygen-containing groups in TBPA form C─O─C bonds with dehydrogenated aromatic molecules in pitch through radical addition, preventing the excessive polycyclic aromatic condensation to reduce the graphitization degree. The benzene ring in TBPA acts as “woundplast” to repair defects in the carbon matrix. This balance between defects and microcrystals will promote sodium storage performance. In contrast, PC with excessive polycondensation of polycyclic aromatic impedes the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202409313-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/83007ee1-c9c3-4339-b8e0-6d416db5afff/smll202409313-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/83007ee1-c9c3-4339-b8e0-6d416db5afff/smll202409313-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b9f50e02-78df-4b12-8514-a90cb5a3fd73/smll202409313-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/83007ee1-c9c3-4339-b8e0-6d416db5afff/smll202409313-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202409313-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202409313" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic comparison of the synthesis strategies of PTC23 with balanced defects and microcrystals and PC with excessive graphitization.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202409313-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202409313-sec-0040-title">2.2 Mechanism Study of Microcrystal Growth Suppression and Defect Repair</h3>
            
            <p>The decomposition, transformation, and cross-linking of precursors determine the microstructure of carbon materials. Thermogravimetry-derivative thermogravimetry (TG-DTG) data reveals the apparent cross-linking between pitch and TBPA (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2a,b</a>). The decomposition and polycondensation of pitch occur in the temperature range of 200–560&nbsp;°C, with the maximum rate of mass loss at 430&nbsp;°C. The carbon yield of pitch is 33.55&nbsp;wt% at 1000&nbsp;°C. TBPA begins to decompose at ≈180&nbsp;°C, reaches its maximum decomposition rate at 290&nbsp;°C, and is almost completely decomposed at 305&nbsp;°C. The theoretical carbon yield of the mixture calculated based on the stoichiometric ratio is 13.6&nbsp;wt% for PTC23, which is much lower than the experimental value (26.69&nbsp;wt%), suggesting the cross-linking between pitch and TBPA.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202409313-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d4163f38-0c6b-49f9-b9e3-927b62564419/smll202409313-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d4163f38-0c6b-49f9-b9e3-927b62564419/smll202409313-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/69fdebe8-505a-4073-a8d2-4872a30ef450/smll202409313-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d4163f38-0c6b-49f9-b9e3-927b62564419/smll202409313-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202409313-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202409313" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Investigation of cross-linking reactions and defect repair processes. a) TG curves, b) DTG curves, and c) DSC curves of pitch, TBPA, and their mixture (the inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2c</a> is an enlargement of zone A). TG-MS data of d) pitch and e) pitch/TBPA mixture. f) XPS C 1s spectrum of PTC23. g) XPS O 1s spectrum of PTC23. h) The contents of oxygen groups. i) Schematic evolution of pitch-derived carbon structure with TBPA regulation (R1 represents the decomposition of TBPA, R2 represents the dehydrogenation of pitch, and R3 represents the complexation process of aromatic molecules with a TBPA-derived product).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In the DSC curve, the endothermic peak at ≈270&nbsp;°C indicates the decomposition of TBPA (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2c</a>). In comparison, the mixture shows almost no endothermic peaks, indicating that the decomposition of TBPA in the presence of pitch is mild and the C─Br bond breakage proceeds gradually, contributing to the subsequent regulation of the condensation behavior of pitch. The pitch displays a notable endothermic peak at ≈480&nbsp;°C, corresponding to the volatilization of aromatic species and the C─H bond breakage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0048_R_d175366166e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Anal. Appl. Pyrol.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The endothermic peak at 560&nbsp;°C (zone A) in the mixture is attributed to the energy expended by the C─H bond breakage in aromatic molecules and the removal of Br atoms during TBPA decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0049_R_d175366166e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Therm. Anal. Calorim.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The dehydrogenated aromatic species show an electron-rich carbon skeleton, which readily forms C─O─C bonds with C═O in TBPA by radical addition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0050_R_d175366166e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chemistry">50</a>-<a href="#smll202409313-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0052_R_d175366166e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Energies">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to the mixture, a more pronounced exothermic peak (zone B) for pitch appears at 700&nbsp;°C, due to the high-degree condensation of aromatic species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0053_R_d175366166e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">53</a>, <a href="#smll202409313-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0054_R_d175366166e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Anal. Appl. Pyrol.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is because of the cross-linking between pitch and TBPA, thus suppressing the aromatic condensation and the graphitization tendency of pitch. The endothermic peak (zone C) of the mixture at 950&nbsp;°C is weaker than that of pitch, implying that a more stable carbon structure is formed by C─O─C bond cross-linking.</p>
            
            <p>The chemical evolution of pitch and pitch/TBPA mixture was real-time studied using TG-MS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2d,e</a>). For pitch, signals of H<sub>2</sub>O, CO, and CH<sub>3</sub>OH appear below 170&nbsp;°C due to the partial decomposition of unstable aromatic molecules in pitch, partial deoxygenation of oxygen-containing groups, and volatilization of the adsorbed water (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2d</a>). Between 560 and 800&nbsp;°C, there is no significant mass loss, suggesting the formation of a relatively stable semi-coke. Above 800&nbsp;°C, the C─O bond activation leads to the formation of CO<sub>2</sub>, as evidenced by the appearance and strengthening of the CO<sub>2</sub> signal. Simultaneously, C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>6</sub> and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub> are observed during the carbonization of pitch. The TG-MS result for the mixture is similar to the pitch below 170&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2e</a>). Above 170&nbsp;°C, the CO signal is intensified due to the decomposition of anhydride in TBPA. Meanwhile, the breakage of the C─Br bond in TBPA leads to a significant increase in the Br<sup>−</sup> signal, and these Br<sup>−</sup> further interact with the H atoms on the aromatic molecules in pitch to form HBr, which etches the carbon structure to form nanopores. It is worth noting that between 170 and 700&nbsp;°C, the H<sub>2</sub>O signal weakens as the Br<sup>−</sup> signal strengthens, implying that Br and O radicals are competitive in reacting with H<sup>+</sup>. The release of Br<sup>−</sup> from the TBPA molecule due to the breakage of the C─Br bond results in positive charges around the carbon atoms on the remaining benzene ring. These Br<sup>−</sup> induces the removal of H atoms from aromatic molecules, causing an electron-rich state at the carbon atom. The positively charged carbon atoms in the TBPA product preferentially bind to the aromatic molecules with electron-rich states, repairing the defects. Meanwhile, the negatively charged aromatic molecules tend to attack the anhydride groups in TBPA derivatives and react with C═O to form C─O─C bonds, thus preventing excessive aromatic condensation. During the condensation of pitch, methyl reacts with the C─O bond in TBPA to produce CH<sub>3</sub>OH.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0055_R_d175366166e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Small">55</a>, <a href="#smll202409313-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0056_R_d175366166e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Fuel">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CO<sub>2</sub> signals are observed in both pitch and mixture above 800&nbsp;°C during the thermal condensation, which favors the pore formation. XPS was employed to analyze the surface chemistry (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2f–h</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Four peaks at 284.2, 284.6, 285.6, and 288.2&nbsp;eV in the C 1s spectrum of PTC23 are attributed to C═C, C─C, C─O, and O─C═O bonds, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2f</a>). In the O 1s spectrum, three peaks at 531.0, 532.0, and 533.2&nbsp;eV belong to O─III (C═O), O─II (C─OH/C─O─C), and O─I (COOH/COOR), respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2g</a>). The contents of different oxygen groups in PC, PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12 are 12.75, 14.59, 14.63, and 8.26 at.%, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2h</a>). With increasing the TBPA/pitch ratio, the total oxygen content rises and then decreases, and PTC23 exhibits the highest oxygen content. This trend is also confirmed by elemental analysis, indicating the highest degree of cross-linking for PTC23 (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, PTC11 has the highest content of C═O bond, while PTC23 has the highest content of C─O─C bond. By reducing the ratio of pitch/TBPA to 2:3, the C═O bond is transformed into a C─O─C bond, and the cross-linking between pitch and TBPA is most adequate through the C─O─C bond. These C─O─C bonds can inhibit the high degree of condensation and stacking of aromatic molecules through the steric hindrance effect, which in turn increases carbon interlayer spacing. A significant decrease in the content of all types of oxygen groups occurs when the ratio is further reduced, implying that excess TBPA impedes cross-linking due to the decomposition and volatilization of unstable oxygen groups. Hence, we propose the balance strategy that the decomposition products of TBPA and pitch are cross-linked by C─O─C bonds and the carbon defect is simultaneously repaired (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0002">2i</a>). The contrast sample (PAC23) was prepared by combining pitch with phthalic anhydride (PA) without Br at the ratio of 2:3. TG curves in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) show the low carbon yield of only 14.40&nbsp;wt%, which is close to the theoretical value of 14.09&nbsp;wt%, indicating the negligible cross-linking. The carbonization yields of PC, PTC11, PTC23, PTC12, and PAC23 were tested to be 56.50%, 73.48%, 84.8%, 90.3%, and 66.93% (calculation based on the mass of pitch to highlight the cross-linking between pitch and TBPA). The carbonization yield of PTC23 was much higher than that of PAC23, which indirectly confirms the importance of the C─Br bond breaking for the cross-linking reaction between the precursors and for the repair of defects. The microcrystalline structure was then examined. XRD peaks at ≈26° for PC and PAC23 indicate the high graphitization degree due to the excessive aromatic condensation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0003">3a</a>). The (002) peaks of PTC samples show apparently weakened intensity and shift toward a smaller diffraction angle, implying the widened interlayer spacing and reduced crystallinity. PTC23 has the largest interlayer spacing of 0.386&nbsp;nm, which is mainly attributed to the fact that the cross-linking reaction between the aromatic molecules and TBPA is the most adequate. According to the (002) peak split-fitting (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0003">3b</a>), PC has the highest proportion of graphite-like phase (92.0%, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0003">3c</a>). The graphite-like phase proportions of PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12 are 29.5%, 31.9%, and 57.9%, respectively, and the gradually enriched graphite-like phase is attributed to the defect repair. PTC23 exhibits a balanced proportion of graphite-like phase (31.9%), pseudo-graphitic phase (45.4%), and highly disordered phase (22.7%). Raman spectra were recorded to study the defect in carbon materials. The D and G bands at 1350 and 1585&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to amorphous and graphitic structures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0003">3d</a>). With increasing the TBPA content, the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio increases and then declines. This is due to the presence of oxygen-containing groups as well as the release of small gas molecules during TBPA decomposition, leading to the defect increase, while the benzene ring in TBPA acts as a “woundplast,” repairing defects and promoting the growth of in-plane grain sizes, and ultimately realizing the balanced coexistence of defects and microcrystals. Excessive repair of defects in carbon structure by TBPA derivatives leads to an increase in the content and size of carbon microcrystals, which is unfavorable for sodium ion storage. According to the Raman spectra fitting results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0003">3e,f</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0019_R_d175366166e887" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#smll202409313-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0057_R_d175366166e887" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Carbon">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> PTC23 exhibits the lowest I<sub>G</sub>/I<sub>D1</sub> value and the highest I<sub>D3</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> and I<sub>D4</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values, suggesting a relatively amorphous structure with a high cross-linking degree. The pore structure and specific surface area were studied. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms show that PTC23 has more micropores than others (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0003">3g</a>). According to the pore size distribution, PTC23 exhibits a narrow pore distribution of 0.7–2&nbsp;nm, favoring the Na<sup>+</sup> storage. SAXS data of materials except PC exhibits a peak at ≈0.2 Å<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0003">3h</a>), suggesting that TBPA promotes the formation of typical pores that are defined as pseudo-closed pores since they are not exactly closed, but only show a small pore entrance size. The pseudo-closed pores are formed by small gas molecules such as HBr and CO<sub>2</sub> etching and contribute to Na<sup>+</sup> storage by pore filling. The true densities of PC, PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12 were tested to be 2.07, 1.83, 1.89, and 1.93&nbsp;g cm<sup>−3</sup>, respectively. The pseudo-closed pore volumes of PC, PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12 were calculated to be 0.041, 0.103, 0.086, and 0.076&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0003">3i</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0058_R_d175366166e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Energy Mater.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The introduction of TBPA significantly decreases the true density, while a further increase leads to a gradual increase in true density, suggesting a decrease in pseudo-closed pore volume. Except for PC, the TBPA/pitch ratio is positively correlated with true density and negatively correlated with pseudo-closed pore volume. PTC23 has a moderate true density and pseudo-closed pore volume, which is because more TBPA will produce more HBr and thus generate more micropores undergoing fusion growth. Furthermore, the benzene ring from TBPA decomposition acts as a “woundplast” to in situ repair the carbon defect, whereas excessive repair results in the growth of carbon microcrystals and blocks the formation of pseudo-closed pores. The microcrystalline structure was studied by HRTEM (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0004">4a–d</a>). Compared to PC without pores, PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12 contain abundant pores. PC contains rich highly-ordered carbon layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0004">4a</a>). Upon introducing TBPA, carbon materials display a disordered microstructure with rich short-range graphite microcrystals, and more TBPA causes the change to long-range graphite domains, which further confirms that TBPA can repair defects and thereby increase the microcrystal size (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0004">4b–d</a>). This agrees well with the in-plane grain sizes (<i>L</i>a) of PTC11 (3.94&nbsp;nm), PTC23 (4.56&nbsp;nm), and PTC12 (4.67&nbsp;nm) as calculated based on XRD data (<b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202409313-tbl-0001">1</a>). PAC23 has a larger grain size (6.30&nbsp;nm) than PC (5.90&nbsp;nm) because PA is difficult to form cross-linking with pitch to prevent condensation of aromatic molecules. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0004">4e</a> schematically illustrates TBPA tuning defects, pore structure, and microcrystalline size in pitch-derived carbon materials. Compared to PC, PTC samples show a larger interlayer spacing (&gt;0.37&nbsp;nm) for sodium ion mass transfer. Since TBPA contains abundant Br atoms, it can promote dehydrogenation to form HBr and produce pores. These pores evolve into pseudo-closed pores after the shrinkage of the pore entrance. Insufficient TBPA hinders the formation of rich pores, while excess TBPA leads to excessive opening of pores and decreases pseudo-closed pores, degrading the sodium storage capacity and ICE. PTC23 exhibits a moderate in-plane grain size (4.56&nbsp;nm), benefitting from the balance between defect repair and microcrystal growth suppression. Excessive repair will result in the overgrowth of grain size, increasing the resistance of ion mass transfer and lowering the reversible storage capacity. Therefore, balancing defects and carbon microcrystals are essential for Na<sup>+</sup> storage in carbon materials.</p>

            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202409313-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202409313-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemistry and Mechanism of Sodium ion Storage</h3>
            
            <p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12 in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) display stronger reduction and oxidation peaks near 0&nbsp;V than PC and PAC23, corresponding to the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrate obvious charge/discharge plateaus below 0.1&nbsp;V for PTC samples compared to PC and PAC23. The reversible specific capacities of PC, PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12 materials are 162.5, 284.5, 336.5, and 291.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5a</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information) provides a comparison of capacity contributed by plateau and sloping regions. The plateau capacity of PC materials only accounts for 10.2%, whereas the value of PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12 increases to 42.7%, 45.6%, and 42.6%, respectively, due to the balanced defects and microcrystals, which promotes the formation of pseudo-closed pores with hybridized carbon microcrystals. The pseudo-closed pore volume is positively related to the pitch/TBPA ratio, while the opposite trend is observed for <i>L</i>a and true density, suggesting that the plateau capacity is closely related to the pseudo-closed pore volume and the in-plane grain size (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5b</a>). PTC23 stands out for its optimal sodium storage performance due to the moderate in-plane grain size, large carbon interlayer spacing, and appropriate pseudo-closed pore volume (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5c</a>; Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12 demonstrate small charge transfer impedances, allowing for good charge transfer (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with other carbon materials reported, PTC23 achieves a higher plateau capacity and a competitive reversible specific capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The CV curves of PTC23 at different scan rates provide insight into the kinetic behavior for sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0059_R_d175366166e1269" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv. Sci.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fitting results imply the diffusion-controlled behavior (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). The contribution ratio of capacitive and diffusion shows that the proportion of capacitive contribution increases with higher scan rates (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) curves and the variation of sodium ion diffusion coefficients of PTC23. A significant decrease of the ion diffusion coefficient is observed when discharging to below 0.1&nbsp;V, followed by an increase, which is attributed to the pore-filling of Na<sup>+</sup> in the pseudo-closed pores, and the charging process exhibits the opposite trend. According to the ex-situ XRD data of PTC23 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5d</a>), the (002) diffraction peak is unchanged above 0.1&nbsp;V because of the only Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on the surface. When discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, Na<sup>+</sup> is inserted into the carbon layer causing the peak to shift leftward.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0028_R_d175366166e1293" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the subsequent discharge, the (002) diffraction peak maintains stable, suggesting the pore-filling type of Na<sup>+</sup> storage. The following charging witnesses the shift back of the (002) peak, suggesting the reversible Na<sup>+</sup> storage. Two new peaks at 39.0° and 56° are attributed to the formation of NaF in solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). Ex-situ Raman (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5e</a>) shows the decreased I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value during the discharge from 3.0 to 0.5&nbsp;V, indicating the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption at the defect sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0021_R_d175366166e1313" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon further discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V, an increase in I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value is observed due to much more disordered microcrystals caused by the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0043_R_d175366166e1324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Storage Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The subsequent discharge to 0.001&nbsp;V leads to a decreased I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value, which is related to the pore-filling of Na<sup>+</sup>. Hence, we proposed the sodium storage mechanism that sodium ions are initially adsorbed at defect sites (sloping region), and then experience the insertion and pore-filling to offer plateau capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5f</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202409313-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3cdca2c1-debf-49c7-a8ab-3b424b83efbf/smll202409313-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3cdca2c1-debf-49c7-a8ab-3b424b83efbf/smll202409313-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fef19af7-bf2e-499c-8d9f-e0a3d079c4e6/smll202409313-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3cdca2c1-debf-49c7-a8ab-3b424b83efbf/smll202409313-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202409313-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202409313" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance and sodium storage mechanism. a) GCD curves at 0.03 A g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Relationship between structural parameters and capacity contributed by the plateau region. c) Cycling performance at 0.3 A g<sup>−1</sup> for PTC11, PTC23, and PTC12. d) The ex-situ XRD patterns of PTC23 electrode. e) The ex-situ Raman spectra of PTC23 electrode during the 2<sup>nd</sup> discharge. f) Schematic diagram of sodium storage mechanism. XPS spectra of g) C 1s, h) O 1s, i) F 1s, and j) Na 1s for PC and PTC23 before and after Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering (5 nm).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We further investigated the SEI chemistry of PC and PTC23 by XPS with depth profiling. In the C 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5g</a>), peaks at 283.9, 284.7, 285.4, 286.3, 287.9, 288.9, and 292.5&nbsp;eV correspond to Na<sub>x</sub>-HC, C═C, C─C, C─O, C═O, RCO<sub>3</sub>Na,&nbsp;and -CF<sub>2</sub>−, respectively. The peaks such as -CF<sub>2</sub>−, RCO<sub>3</sub>Na, C═O, and C─O are attributed to the decomposition of diglyme solvent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202409313-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202409313-bib-0060_R_d175366166e1384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with PTC23, an apparent decrease of RCO<sub>3</sub>Na signal is observed after sputtering (5 nm) of PC, because of the smaller specific surface area of PC. The stronger Na<sub>x</sub>-HC peak signal of PTC23 suggests a higher degree of sodiation. In the O 1s spectra, the O─F (≈535.0&nbsp;eV), O─C/O─H (≈531.2&nbsp;eV), and C═O/Na─O (≈530.2&nbsp;eV) peaks are attributed to the reduction of NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5h</a>). After sputtering of PC and PTC23, the signal of C─F (≈688.0&nbsp;eV) is weakened (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5i</a>), while the signal of Na─F (≈683.0&nbsp;eV) becomes stronger, implying the SEI chemistry of an organic outer layer and an inorganic inner layer such as NaF. In addition, the Na─F signal of PTC23 is significantly weaker than that of PC, whereas the Na 1s spectra of PTC23 in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202409313-fig-0005">5j</a> show a NaF/Na─O (≈1069.0&nbsp;eV) signal intensity similar to that of PC, implying that the SEI of PTC23 may contain more inorganic substances like Na<sub>2</sub>O alongside fewer F-containing substances. These results indicate that the SEI on the electrode surface consists of an organic outer layer featured by -CF<sub>2</sub>-, RCO<sub>3</sub>Na, etc., and an inorganic inner layer rich in NaF, Na<sub>2</sub>O, etc. This organic/inorganic composite SEI is beneficial to the electrode/electrolyte interface stability.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202409313-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202409313-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We report a defect and microcrystal balance method enabled by molecular chemical engineering to improve the sodium storage performance of carbon anodes. The decomposition of TBPA molecule can induce the dehydrogenation of pitch to release small gas molecules as well as the cross-linking of the C═O bonds in TBPA with the dehydrogenated pitch to form a C─O─C bond, which can prevent the aromatic molecules in pitch from over polymerization and thus inhibit the excessive growth of microcrystals. Meanwhile, the in-situ repair of defects is realized by the combination of decomposition products with dehydrogenated pitch after the removal of Br<sup>−</sup> in TBPA. Key parameters such as <i>L</i>a, pseudo-closed pore volume, true density, and I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> under different raw material ratios are systematically examined and compared to confirm the proposed balanced mechanism of defect repair and microcrystal growth suppression. In particular, the optimal sample (PTC23), which realizes a balanced microstructure, has a large interlayer spacing (0.386&nbsp;nm), moderate in-plane grain size (4.56&nbsp;nm), and pseudo-closed pore volume (0.086&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) with a capacity of 336.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The sodium ion storage mechanism, involving “adsorption-insertion-pore filling”, is elucidated through ex-situ XRD and ex-situ Raman. This defect and microcrystal balance method highlights the comprehensive regulation of carbon anodes rather than a single factor and provides a molecular chemical engineering approach to carbon material design toward the application in SIBs and other batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
